THE PRO- Â´ETALE TOPOLOGY FOR SCHEMES
BHARGAV BHATT AND PETER SCHOLZE
To GÂ´erard Laumon, with respect and admiration
ABSTRACT. We give a new deï¬nition of the derived category of constructible Qâ„“-sheaves on a scheme, which
is as simple as the geometric intuition behind them. Moreover, we deï¬ne a reï¬ned fundamental group of
schemes, which is large enough to see all lisse Qâ„“-sheaves, even on non-normal schemes. To accomplish these
tasks, we deï¬ne and study the pro-Â´etale topology, which is a Grothendieck topology on schemes that is closely
related to the Â´etale topology, and yet better suited for inï¬nite constructions typically encountered in â„“-adic
cohomology. An essential foundational result is that this site is locally contractible in a well-deï¬ned sense.

1. INTRODUCTION
Let X be a variety over an algebraically closed ï¬eld k. The Â´etale cohomology groups Hi(XÂ´et, Qâ„“), where
â„“is a prime different from the characteristic of k, are of fundamental importance in algebraic geometry.
Unfortunately, the standard deï¬nition of these groups is somewhat indirect. Indeed, contrary to what the
notation suggests, these groups are not obtained as the cohomology of a sheaf Qâ„“on the Â´etale site XÂ´et. The
Â´etale site gives the correct answer only with torsion coefï¬cients, so the correct deï¬nition is
Hi(XÂ´et, Qâ„“) := (lim

Hi(XÂ´et, Z/â„“nZ)) âŠ—Zâ„“Qâ„“.
In this simple situation, this technical point is often unproblematic1. However, even here, it takes effort to
construct a natural commutative differential graded Qâ„“-algebra giving rise to these cohomology groups. This
so-called Qâ„“-homotopy type was constructed by Deligne in [Del80], using certain subtle integral aspects of
homotopy theory due independently to Miller [Mil78] and Grothendieck.
For more sophisticated applications, however, it is important to work in a relative setup (i.e., study con-
structible sheaves), and keep track of the objects in the derived category, instead of merely the cohomology
groups. In other words, one wants a well-behaved derived category Db
c(X, Qâ„“) of constructible Qâ„“-sheaves.
Deligne, [Del80], and in greater generality Ekedahl, [Eke90], showed that it is possible to deï¬ne such a
category along the lines of the deï¬nition of Hi(XÂ´et, Qâ„“). Essentially, one replaces Hi(XÂ´et, Z/â„“nZ) with
the derived category Db

Needless to say, this presentation is oversimpliï¬ed, and veils substantial technical difï¬culties.
Nonetheless, in daily life, one pretends (without getting into much trouble) that Db
c(X, Qâ„“) is simply
the full subcategory of some hypothetical derived category D(X, Qâ„“) of all Qâ„“-sheaves spanned by those
bounded complexes whose cohomology sheaves are locally constant along a stratiï¬cation. Our goal in this
paper to justify this intuition, by showing that the following deï¬nitions recover the classical notions. To
state them, we need the pro-Â´etale site XproÂ´et, which is introduced below. For any topological space T, one
has a â€˜constantâ€™ sheaf on XproÂ´et associated with T; in particular, there is a sheaf of (abstract) rings Qâ„“on

Deï¬nition 1.1. Let X be a scheme whose underlying topological space is noetherian.
(1) A sheaf L of Qâ„“-modules on XproÂ´et is lisse if it is locally free of ï¬nite rank.
(2) A sheaf C of Qâ„“-modules on XproÂ´et is constructible if there is a ï¬nite stratiï¬cation {Xi â†’X} into
locally closed subsets Xi âŠ‚X such that C|Xi is lisse.
(3) An object K âˆˆD(XproÂ´et, Qâ„“) is constructible if it is bounded, and all cohomology sheaves are
constructible. Let Db
c(X, Qâ„“) âŠ‚D(XproÂ´et, Qâ„“) be the corresponding full triangulated subcategory.
The formalism of the six functors is easily described in this setup. In particular, in the setup above, with
the naive interpretation of the right-hand side, one has
Hi(XÂ´et, Qâ„“) = Hi(XproÂ´et, Qâ„“) ;
for general X, one recovers Jannsenâ€™s continuous Â´etale cohomology, [Jan88].
Similarly, the complex
RÎ“(XproÂ´et, Qâ„“) is obtained by literally applying the derived functor RÎ“(XproÂ´et, âˆ’) to a sheaf of Q-algebras,
and hence naturally has the structure of a commutative differential graded algebra by general nonsense (see
[Ols11, Â§2] for example); this gives a direct construction of the Qâ„“-homotopy type in complete generality.
A version of the pro-Â´etale site was deï¬ned in [Sch13] in the context of adic spaces. The deï¬nition given
there was somewhat artiï¬cial, mostly because non-noetherian adic spaces are not in general well-behaved.
This is not a concern in the world of schemes, so one can give a very simple and natural deï¬nition of XproÂ´et.
Until further notice, X is allowed to be an arbitrary scheme.

Deï¬nition 1.2.
(1) A map f : Y â†’X of schemes is weakly Â´etale if f is ï¬‚at and âˆ†f : Y â†’Y Ã—X Y is ï¬‚at.
(2) The pro-Â´etale site XproÂ´et is the site of weakly Â´etale X-schemes, with covers given by fpqc covers.
Any map between weakly Â´etale X-schemes is itself weakly Â´etale, and the resulting topos has good cate-
gorical properties, like coherence (if X is qcqs) and (hence) existence of enough points. For this deï¬nition
to be useful, however, we need to control the class of weakly Â´etale morphisms. In this regard, we prove the
following theorem.
Theorem 1.3. Let f : A â†’B be a map of rings.
(1) f is Â´etale if and only if f is weakly Â´etale and ï¬nitely presented.
(2) If f is ind-Â´etale, i.e. B is a ï¬ltered colimit of Â´etale A-algebras, then f is weakly Â´etale.
(3) If f is weakly Â´etale, then there exists a faithfully ï¬‚at ind-Â´etale g : B â†’C such that g â—¦f is ind-Â´etale.
In other words, for a ring A, the sites deï¬ned by weakly Â´etale A-algebras and by ind-Â´etale A-algebras are
equivalent, which justiï¬es the name pro-Â´etale site for the site XproÂ´et deï¬ned above. We prefer using weakly
Â´etale morphisms to deï¬ne XproÂ´et as the property of being weakly Â´etale is clearly Â´etale local on the source
and target, while that of being ind-Â´etale is not even Zariski local on the target.
One might worry that the pro-Â´etale site is huge in an uncontrolled way (e.g., covers might be too large,
introducing set-theoretic problems). However, this does not happen. To see this, we need a deï¬nition:
Deï¬nition 1.4. An afï¬ne scheme U is w-contractible if any faithfully ï¬‚at weakly Â´etale map V â†’U admits
a section.
A w-contractible object U âˆˆXproÂ´et is somewhat analogous to a point in the topos theoretic sense: the
functor Î“(U, âˆ’) is exact and commutes with all limits, rather than colimits. In fact, a geometric point of
X deï¬nes a w-contractible object in XproÂ´et via the strict henselisation. However, there are many more
w-contractible objects, which is the key to the control alluded to above:
Theorem 1.5. Any scheme X admits a cover in XproÂ´et by w-contractible afï¬ne schemes.
Despite the analogy between w-contractible objects and points, Theorem 1.5 has stronger consequences
than the mere existence of points. For example, the inverse limit functor on systems
. . . â†’Fn â†’Fnâˆ’1 â†’. . . â†’F1 â†’F0
of sheaves on XproÂ´et is well-behaved, the derived category of abelian sheaves on XproÂ´et is left-complete and
compactly generated, unbounded cohomological descent holds in the derived category, and Postnikov towers
converge in the hypercomplete âˆ-topos associated with XproÂ´et. This shows that the pro-Â´etale site is useful
even when working with torsion coefï¬cients, as the derived category of XÂ´et is left-complete (and unbounded
cohomological descent holds) only under ï¬niteness assumptions on the cohomological dimension of X, cf.
[LO08].
We note that one can â€˜cut offâ€™ XproÂ´et by only allowing weakly Â´etale X-schemes Y of cardinality < Îº
for some uncountable strong limit cardinal Îº > |X|, and all results above, especially the existence of w-
contractible covers, remain true. In particular, the resulting truncated site XproÂ´et forms a set, rather than a
proper class, so we can avoid universes in this paper.
Let us explain the local structure of a scheme in the pro-Â´etale site.
Deï¬nition 1.6.
(1) A ring A is w-local if the subset (SpecA)c âŠ‚SpecA of closed points is closed, and any connected
component of SpecA has a unique closed point.
(2) A map f : A â†’B of w-local rings is w-local if Specf : SpecB â†’SpecA maps closed points to
closed points.
The next result shows that every scheme is covered by w-local afï¬nes in the pro-Zariski topology, and
hence in the pro-Â´etale topology. In particular, as noetherian schemes have ï¬nitely many connected compo-
nents, this shows that non-noetherian schemes are unavoidable when studying XproÂ´et, even for X noetherian.

Theorem 1.7. The inclusion of the category of w-local rings with w-local maps in the category of all
rings admits a left adjoint A 7â†’AZ. The unit A â†’AZ of the adjunction is faithfully ï¬‚at and an ind-
(Zariski localisation), so SpecAZ â†’SpecA is a cover in Spec(A)proÂ´et. Moreover, the subset (SpecAZ)c âŠ‚
SpecAZ of closed points maps homeomorphically to SpecA, equipped with its constructible topology.
In other words, SpecAZ is roughly the disjoint union of the local rings of A. However, the union is not
exactly disjoint; rather, the set of connected components Ï€0(SpecAZ) is naturally a proï¬nite set, which is
SpecA with its constructible topology. Thus, the study of w-local rings splits into the study of its local rings
at closed points, and the study of proï¬nite sets. It turns out in practice that these two aspects interact little.
In particular, this leads to the following characterization of w-contractible schemes.
Theorem 1.8. An afï¬ne scheme X = SpecA is w-contractible if and only if A is w-local, all local rings at
closed points are strictly henselian, and Ï€0(X) is extremally disconnected.
Recall that a proï¬nite set S is extremally disconnected if the closure of any open subset U âŠ‚S is still
open. By a theorem of Gleason, S is extremally disconnected if and only if S is projective in the category
of compact Hausdorff spaces, i.e., any surjective map T â†’S from a compact Hausdorff space T admits a
section. In particular, the Stone-Cech compactiï¬cation of any discrete set is extremally disconnected, which
proves the existence of enough such spaces. Using this construction, if A is w-local, it is relatively easy to
construct a faithfully ï¬‚at ind-Â´etale A-algebra B satisfying the conditions of the theorem, which proves the
existence of enough w-contractible schemes.

Finally, let us give a short summary of the different sections. In Section 2, we study w-local rings and
the like. In Section 3, we study a general topos-theoretic notion (namely, repleteness) which implies left-
completeness of the derived category etc. . We also include some discussions on complete sheaves, which
are again well-behaved under the assumption of repleteness. In Section 4, we introduce the pro-Â´etale site,
and study its basic properties. The relation with the Â´etale site is studied in detail in Section 5. In Section 6, we
introduce constructible sheaves (recalling ï¬rst the theory for torsion coefï¬cients on the Â´etale site), showing
that for schemes whose underlying topological space is noetherian, one gets the very simple deï¬nition stated
above. Finally, in Section 7, we deï¬ne the pro-Â´etale fundamental group.

2. LOCAL STRUCTURE
The goal of this section is to study some algebra relevant to the pro-Â´etale topology. Speciï¬cally, we show:

2.1. Spectral spaces. Let S be the category of spectral spaces with spectral maps, and let Sf âŠ‚S be the full
subcategory of ï¬nite spectral spaces (= ï¬nite T0 spaces), so S = Pro(Sf), cf. [Hoc69]. Our main goal is to
show that each X âˆˆS admits a pro-(open cover) XZ â†’X such that XZ admits no further non-split open
covers. This goal is eventually realized in Lemma 2.1.10. Before constructing XZ, however, we introduce
and study the subcategory of S where spaces of the form XZ live:
Deï¬nition 2.1.1. A spectral space X is w-local if it satisï¬es:
(1) All open covers split, i.e., for every open cover {Ui Ö’â†’X}, the map âŠ”iUi â†’X has a section.
(2) The subspace Xc âŠ‚X of closed points is closed.
A map f : X â†’Y of w-local spaces is w-local if f is spectral and f(Xc) âŠ‚Y c. Let i : Swl Ö’â†’S be the
subcategory of w-local spaces with w-local maps.
The ï¬rst condition in Deï¬nition 2.1.1 is obviously necessary for the promised application. The second
condition turns out to be particularly convenient for applications.
Example 2.1.2. Any proï¬nite set is a w-local space. Any local scheme has a w-local topological space. The
collection of w-local spaces is closed under ï¬nite disjoint unions.
The property of w-locality passes to closed subspaces:
Lemma 2.1.3. If X âˆˆSwl, and Z âŠ‚X is closed, then Z âˆˆSwl.
Proof. Open covers of Z split as any open cover of Z extends to one of X (by extending opens and adding
X âˆ’Z). Moreover, it is clear that Zc = Xc âˆ©Z, so the claim follows.

Recall that the inclusion Pro(Setf) âŠ‚Pro(Sf) = S has a left-adjoint X 7â†’Ï€0(X), i.e., the counit X â†’

Proof. The second part follows immediately from the ï¬rst as Xc is proï¬nite when X is w-local. For the

2.2. Rings. We now adapt the notions of Â§2.1 to the world of rings via the Zariski topology, and also discuss

Deï¬nition 2.2.1. Fix a ring A.

Example 2.2.2. For any ring A, there is an ind-(Zariski localization) A â†’AZ such that Spec(AZ) =

Our goal in this section is to explain why every ring admits an ind-Â´etale faithfully ï¬‚at w-strictly local
algebra. The construction of this extension, very roughly, mirrors the classical construction of the strict

Lemma 2.2.3. If A is w-local, then the Jacobson radical IA cuts out Spec(A)c âŠ‚Spec(A) with its reduced
structure. The quotient A/IA is an absolutely ï¬‚at ring.
Recall that a ring B is called absolutely ï¬‚at if B is reduced with Krull dimension 0 (or, equivalently, that
B is reduced with Spec(B) Hausdorff).
Proof. Let J âŠ‚A be the (radical) ideal cutting out Spec(A)c âŠ‚Spec(A) with the reduced structure. Then
J âŠ‚m for each m âˆˆSpec(A)c, so J âŠ‚IA. Hence, Spec(A/IA) âŠ‚Spec(A)c is a closed subspace; we

2.3. Weakly Â´etale versus pro-Â´etale. In this section, we study the following notion:
Deï¬nition 2.3.1. A morphism A â†’B of commutative rings is called weakly Â´etale if both A â†’B and the
multiplication morphism B âŠ—A B â†’B are ï¬‚at.
Remark 2.3.2. Weakly Â´etale morphisms have been studied previously in the literature under the name of
absolutely ï¬‚at morphisms, see [Oli72]. Here, we follow the terminology introduced in [GR03, Deï¬nition
3.1.1].
Our goal in this section is to show that weakly Â´etale maps and ind-Â´etale maps generate the same Grothendieck
topology, see Theorem 2.3.4 below. We begin by recording basic properties of weakly Â´etale maps.
11

Proposition 2.3.3. Fix maps f : A â†’B, g : B â†’C, and h : A â†’D of rings.
(1) If f is ind-Â´etale, then f is weakly Â´etale.
(2) If f is weakly Â´etale, then the cotangent complex LB/A vanishes. In particular, f is formally Â´etale.
(3) If f is weakly Â´etale and ï¬nitely presented, then f is Â´etale.
(4) If f and g are weakly Â´etale (resp. ind-Â´etale), then g â—¦f is weakly Â´etale (resp. ind-Â´etale). If g â—¦f and
f are weakly Â´etale (resp. ind-Â´etale), then g is weakly Â´etale (resp. ind-Â´etale).
(5) If h is faithfully ï¬‚at, then f is weakly Â´etale if and only if f âŠ—A D : D â†’B âŠ—A D is weakly Â´etale.
Proof. These are well-known, so we mostly give references.
(1) As ï¬‚atness and tensor products are preserved under ï¬ltered colimits, one reduces to the case of Â´etale
morphisms. Clearly, f is ï¬‚at in that case; moreover, B âŠ—A B â†’B is an open immersion on spectra,
and in particular ï¬‚at.
(2) See [GR03, Theorem 2.5.36] and [GR03, Proposition 3.2.16].
(3) Since f is weakly Â´etale and ï¬nitely presented, it is formally Â´etale and ï¬nitely presented by (2), hence
Â´etale.
(4) The ï¬rst part is clear. For the second part in the weakly Â´etale case, see [GR03, Lemma 3.1.2 (iv)].
For the ind-Â´etale case, observe that the category of ind-Â´etale algebras is equivalent to the ind-category
of Â´etale algebras by ï¬nite presentation constraints.
(5) This is clear, as ï¬‚atness can be checked after a faithfully ï¬‚at base change.
â–¡
The analogue of (5) fails for ind-Â´etale morphisms. Our main result in this section is:
Theorem 2.3.4. Let f : A â†’B be weakly Â´etale. Then there exists a faithfully ï¬‚at ind-Â´etale morphism
g : B â†’C such that g â—¦f : A â†’C is ind-Â´etale.
The local version of Theorem 2.3.4 follows from the following result of Olivier, [Oli72]:
Theorem 2.3.5 (Olivier). Let A be a strictly henselian local ring, and let B be a weakly Â´etale local A-
algebra. Then f : A â†’B is an isomorphism.
Remark 2.3.6. One might hope to use Theorem 2.3.5 for a direct proof of Theorem 2.3.4: Assume that
f : A â†’B is weakly Â´etale. Let C = Q
x Afâˆ—x, where x runs over a set of representatives for the geometric

However, although each Ax is ind-Â´etale over A, C is not even weakly Â´etale over A, as inï¬nite products do
not preserve ï¬‚atness. In order to make the argument work, one would have to replace the inï¬nite product

factor over a ï¬nitely presented A-subalgebra of Ax, one could also make the argument work. However, in
the absence of any ï¬niteness conditions, this is not possible.

2.4. Local contractibility. In this section, we study the following notion:
Deï¬nition 2.4.1. A ring A is w-contractible if every faithfully ï¬‚at ind-Â´etale map A â†’B has a section.
The name â€œw-contractibleâ€ is inspired by the connection with the pro-Â´etale topology: if A is w-contractible,
then Spec(A) admits no non-split pro-Â´etale covers, and is hence a â€œweakly contractibleâ€ object of the cor-
responding topos. Our goal is to prove that every ring admits a w-contractible ind-Â´etale faithfully ï¬‚at cover.
We begin by observing that w-contractible rings are already w-local:
Lemma 2.4.2. A w-contractible ring A is w-local (and thus w-strictly local).
Proof. The map Ï€ : Spec(AZ) â†’Spec(A) has a section s by the assumption on A. The section s is a
closed immersion since Ï€ is separated, and Spec(AZ) = Spec(A)Z is w-local, so we are done by Lemma
2.1.3.
â–¡
The notion of w-contractibility is local along a henselian ideal:
Lemma 2.4.3. Let A be a ring henselian along an ideal I. Then A is w-contractible if and only if A/I is
so.
Proof. This is proven exactly like Lemma 2.2.13 using that Ind(AÂ´et) â†’Ind((A/I)Â´et) is essentially surjec-
tive, and preserves and reï¬‚ects faithfully ï¬‚at maps.
â–¡
The main difference between w-contractible and w-strictly local rings lies in the topology. To give mean-
ing to this phrase, recall the following deï¬nition:
13

Deï¬nition 2.4.4. A compact Hausdorff space is extremally disconnected if the closure of every open is open.
One has the following result characterizing such spaces, see [Gle58]:
Theorem 2.4.5 (Gleason). Extremally disconnected spaces are exactly the projective objects in the category
of all compact Hausdorff spaces, i.e., those X for which every continuous surjection Y â†’X splits.
It is fairly easy to prove the existence of â€œenoughâ€ extremally disconnected spaces:
Example 2.4.6. For any set X, given the discrete topology, the Stone-Cech compactiï¬cation Î²(X) is ex-
tremally disconnected: the universal property shows that Î²(X) is a projective object in the category of com-
pact Hausdorff spaces. If X itself comes from a compact Hausdorff space, then the counit map Î²(X) â†’X
is a continuous surjection, which shows that all compact Hausdorff spaces can be covered by extremally
disconnected spaces. In fact, the same argument shows that any extremally disconnected space is a retract
of Î²(X) for some set X.
Extremally disconnected spaces tend to be quite large, as the next example shows:
Example 2.4.7. An elementary argument due to Gleason shows that any convergent sequence in an ex-
tremally disconnected space is eventually constant. It follows that standard proï¬nite sets, such as Zp (or the
Cantor set) are not extremally disconnected.
The relevance of extremally disconnected spaces for us is:
Lemma 2.4.8. A w-strictly local ring A is w-contractible if and only if Ï€0(Spec(A)) is extremally discon-
nected.
Proof. As Spec(A)c â†’Spec(A) gives a section of Spec(A) â†’Ï€0(Spec(A)), if A is w-contractible, then
every continuous surjection T â†’Ï€0(Spec(A)) of proï¬nite sets has a section, so Ï€0(Spec(A)) is extremally
disconnected. Conversely, assume A is w-strictly local and Ï€0(Spec(A)) is extremally disconnected. By
Lemma 2.4.3, we may assume A = A/IA. Thus, we must show: if A is an absolutely ï¬‚at ring whose local
rings are separably closed ï¬elds, and Spec(A) is extremally disconnected, then A is w-contractible. Pick
an ind-Â´etale faithfully ï¬‚at A-algebra B. Then A â†’B induces an isomorphism on local rings. Lemma
2.2.6 gives a factorization A â†’C â†’B with A â†’C a ind-(Zariski localization) induced by a map of
proï¬nite sets T â†’Spec(A), and B â†’C a w-local map inducing an isomorphism on spectra. Then C â‰ƒB
as the local rings of C and B coincide with those of A. As Spec(A) is extremally disconnected, the map
T â†’Spec(A) of proï¬nite sets has a section s. The closed subscheme Spec(Câ€²) âŠ‚Spec(C) realizing
s(Spec(A)) âŠ‚T maps isomorphically to Spec(A), which gives the desired section.
â–¡
We now show the promised covers exist:
Lemma 2.4.9. For any ring A, there is an ind-Â´etale faithfully ï¬‚at A-algebra Aâ€² with Aâ€² w-contractible.

Lemma 2.4.10. Let A be a w-contractible ring, and let f : A â†’B be a ï¬nite ring map of ï¬nite presentation.

3. ON REPLETE TOPOI
A topos is the category of sheaves on a site, up to equivalence, as in [SGA72a]. We will study in Â§3.1 a
general property of topoi that implies good behaviour for the lim and R lim functors, as well as unbounded
cohomological descent, as discussed in Â§3.3. A special subclass of such topoi with even better completeness
properties is isolated in Â§3.2; this class is large enough for all applications later in the paper. In Â§3.4 and
Â§3.5, with a view towards studying complexes of â„“-adic sheaves on the pro-Â´etale site, we study derived
completions of rings and modules in a replete topos; the repleteness ensures no interference from higher
derived limits while performing completions, so the resulting theory is as good as in the punctual case.
3.1. Deï¬nition and ï¬rst consequences. The key deï¬nition is:
Deï¬nition 3.1.1. A topos X is replete if surjections in X are closed under sequential limits, i.e., if F :
Nop â†’X is a diagram with Fn+1 â†’Fn surjective for all n, then lim F â†’Fn is surjective for each n.
Before giving examples, we mention two recogition mechanisms for replete topoi:
Lemma 3.1.2. If X is a replete topos and X âˆˆX, then X/X is replete.
Proof. This follows from the fact that the forgetful functor X/X â†’X commutes with connected limits and
preserves surjections.
â–¡
Lemma 3.1.3. A topos X is replete if and only if there exists a surjection X â†’1 and X/X is replete.
Proof. This follows from two facts: (a) limits commute with limits, and (b) a map F â†’G in X is a
surjection if and only if it is so after base changing to X.
â–¡
Example 3.1.4. The topos of sets is replete, and hence so is the topos of presheaves on a small category.
As a special case, the classifying topos of a ï¬nite group G (which is simply the category of presheaves on
B(G)) is replete.
Example 3.1.5. Let k be a ï¬eld with a ï¬xed separable closure k. Then X = Shv(Spec(k)Â´et) is replete if
and only if k is a ï¬nite extension of k.3 One direction is clear: if k/k is ï¬nite, then Spec(k) covers the
ï¬nal object of X and X/Spec(k) â‰ƒSet, so X is replete by Lemma 3.1.3. Conversely, assume that X is replete
with k/k inï¬nite. Then there is a tower k = k0 Ö’â†’k1 Ö’â†’k2 Ö’â†’. . . of strictly increasing ï¬nite separable
extensions of k. The associated diagram Â· Â· Â· â†’Spec(k2) â†’Spec(k1) â†’Spec(k0) of surjections has an
empty limit in X, contradicting repleteness.
Remark 3.1.6. Replacing Nop with an arbitrary small coï¬ltered category in the deï¬nition of replete topoi
leads to an empty theory: there are coï¬ltered diagrams of sets with surjective transition maps and empty
limits. For example, consider the poset I of ï¬nite subsets of an uncountable set T ordered by inclusion, and

schemes fail repleteness due to â€œï¬nite presentationâ€ constraints. Nevertheless, there is an interesting geo-
metric source of examples:
Example 3.1.7. The topos X of fpqc sheaves on the category of schemes4 is replete. Given a diagram

Question 3.1.12. Do Postnikov towers converge in the hypercomplete âˆ-topos of sheaves of spaces (as in

3.2. Locally weakly contractible topoi. We brieï¬‚y study an exceptionally well-behaved subclass of re-

Deï¬nition 3.2.1. An object F of a topos X is called weakly contractible if every surjection G â†’F has a

Example 3.2.2. The topos X = Set is locally weakly contractible: the singleton set S is weakly contractible
coherent, and every set is covered by a disjoint union of copies of S.

Proposition 3.2.3. Let X be a locally weakly contractible topos. Then
(1) X is replete.
(2) The derived category D(X) = D(X, Z) is compactly generated.
(3) Postnikov towers converge in the associated hypercomplete âˆ-topos. (Cf. [Lur09].)
Proof. For (1), note that a map F â†’G in X is surjective if and only if F(Y ) â†’G(Y ) is so for each weakly

proving the claim. For (3), ï¬rst note that the functor F 7â†’F(Y ) is exact on sheaves of spaces whenever Y
is weakly contractible. Hence, given such an F and point âˆ—âˆˆF(Y ) with Y weakly contractible, one has

[Lur09, Proposition 7.2.1.10].)

Proof. We freely use that homotopy-limits and homotopy-colimits in D(Xâ€¢) are computed â€œtermwise.â€

Deï¬nition 3.4.1. We say that M âˆˆModR is classically I-complete if M â‰ƒlim M/InM; write ModR,comp âŠ‚

Proposition 3.4.2. An R-module M âˆˆModR is classically I-complete if and only if it is I-adically sepa-

ring R with principal ideals I and J such that R is classically I-complete while the quotient R/J is not
I-adically separated; note that R/J = cok(R â†’R) is derived I-complete by Lemma 3.4.14.
The result for complexes is:
Proposition 3.4.4. An R-complex K âˆˆD(X, R) is derived I-complete if and only if each Hi(K) is so.

4. THE PRO-Â´ETALE TOPOLOGY
We deï¬ne the pro-Â´etale site of a scheme in Â§4.1, and study the associated topos in Â§4.2. In Â§4.3, we use
these ideas to construct a variant of Tateâ€™s continuous cohomology of proï¬nite groups that behaves better in
some functorial respects.
4.1. The site.
Deï¬nition 4.1.1. A map f : Y â†’X of schemes is called weakly Â´etale if f is ï¬‚at and âˆ†f : Y â†’Y Ã—X Y
is ï¬‚at. Write XproÂ´et for the category of weakly Â´etale X-schemes, which we give the structure of a site by
declaring a cover to be one that is a cover in the fpqc topology, i.e. a family {Yi â†’Y } of maps in XproÂ´et is
a covering family if any open afï¬ne in Y is mapped onto by an open afï¬ne in âŠ”iYi.
Remark 4.1.2. To avoid set-theoretic issues, it sufï¬ces for our purposes to deï¬ne the site XproÂ´et using
weakly Â´etale maps Y â†’X with |Y | < Îº, where Îº is a ï¬xed uncountable strong limit cardinal larger
than |X|.5 The choice of Îº is dictated by the desire to have Shv(XproÂ´et) be locally weakly contractible.
Increasing Îº results in a different topos, but cohomology remains the same, as it can be calculated by a
simplicial covering with w-contractible schemes.
Remark 4.1.3. We do not directly work with pro-Â´etale morphisms of schemes to deï¬ne XproÂ´et as the
property of being pro-Â´etale is not geometric: Example 4.1.12 shows its failure to localise on the target.
Nonetheless, we call XproÂ´et the pro-Â´etale site, as by Theorem 2.3.4 any weakly Â´etale map f : Y â†’X is
Zariski locally on X and locally in YproÂ´et of the form SpecB â†’SpecA with A â†’B ind-Â´etale.
Some elementary examples of weakly Â´etale maps:
Example 4.1.4. For a ï¬eld k, a map Spec(R) â†’Spec(k) is weakly Â´etale if and only if k â†’R is ind-Â´etale.
Indeed, R embeds into some ind-Â´etale k-algebra S; but one checks easily that as k is a ï¬eld, any subalgebra
of an ind-Â´etale k-algebra is again ind-Â´etale.
Example 4.1.5. For a scheme X and a geometric point x, the map Spec(Osh

Lemma 4.1.6. Compositions and base changes of weakly Â´etale maps are weakly Â´etale.
Proof. Clear.

In fact, more generally, one can deï¬ne â€™constant sheavesâ€™ associated with topological spaces. Indeed, let
X be any scheme, and let T be some topological space.
Lemma 4.2.12. The association mapping any U âˆˆXproÂ´et to Mapcont(U, T) is a sheaf FT on XproÂ´et. If
T is totally disconnected and U is qcqs, then FT (U) = Mapcont(Ï€0(U), T). In particular, if T is discrete,
then FT is the constant sheaf associated with T.
Proof. To show that FT is a sheaf, one reduces to proving that if f : A â†’B is a faithfully ï¬‚at ind-Â´etale
morphism of rings, then M âŠ‚SpecA is open if and only if (Specf)âˆ’1(M) âŠ‚SpecB is open. Only the
converse is nontrivial, so assume (Specf)âˆ’1(M) âŠ‚SpecB is open. First, we claim that M is open in
the constructible topology. Indeed, the map Specf : SpecB â†’SpecA is a continuous map of compact
Hausdorff spaces when considering the constructible topologies. In particular, it is closed, so
SpecA \ M = (Specf)(SpecB \ (Specf)âˆ’1(M))
is closed, and thus M is open (in the constructible topology). To check that M is actually open, it is enough
to verify that M is closed under generalizations. This is clear, as Specf is generalizing, and (Specf)âˆ’1(M)
is open (and thus closed under generalizations).
If T is totally disconnected and U is qcqs, then any continuous map U â†’T will necessarily factor

6. CONSTRUCTIBLE SHEAVES
This long section studies constructible sheaves, with the ultimate goal of giving a different perspective on
the notion of a Qâ„“-sheaf. We begin by studying in Â§6.1 and Â§6.2 the basic functoriality of pushforward and
pullback along locally closed immersions; the main novelty here is that pullback along a closed immersion
is limit- and colimit-preserving, contrary to the classical story. Next, we recall the theory of constructible
complexes in the Â´etale topology in Â§6.3. We alert the reader that our deï¬nition of constructibility is more
natural from the derived perspective, but not the usual one: a constructible complex on a geometric point is
the same thing as a perfect complex, see Remark 6.3.2. In particular, the truncation operators Ï„â‰¥n, Ï„â‰¤n do
not in general preserve constructibility. As a globalisation of this remark, we detour in Â§6.4 to prove that
constructible complexes are the same as compact objects under a suitable ï¬niteness constraint; this material
is surely standard, but not easy to ï¬nd in the literature. We then introduce constructible complexes in the
pro-Â´etale world in Â§6.5 with coefï¬cients in a complete noetherian local ring (R, m) as those R-complexes
on XproÂ´et which are complete (in the sense of Â§3.4), and classically constructible modulo m. This deï¬nition
is well-suited for comparison with the classical picture, but, as we explain in Â§6.6, also coincides with the
more intuitive deï¬nition on a noetherian scheme: a constructible complex is simply an R-complex that is
locally constant and perfect along a stratiï¬cation. This perspective leads in Â§6.8 to a direct construction of
the category of constructible complexes over coefï¬cient rings that do not satisfy the above constraints, like
Zâ„“and Qâ„“. Along the way, we establish that the formalism of the 6 functors â€œworksâ€ in this setting in Â§6.7.

Remark 6.4.7. The constructibility of Z in Lemma 6.4.6 is necessary. For a counterexample without this

simply tensors this example with a geometric point, in the sense of Example 4.1.9.

To state our result, we need the following deï¬nition.
Deï¬nition 6.6.9. A scheme X is said to be topologically noetherian if its underlying topological space is
noetherian, i.e. any descending sequence of closed subsets is eventually constant.
Lemma 6.6.10. Let T be a topological space.
(1) If T is noetherian, then T is qcqs and has only ï¬nitely many connected components. Moreover, any
locally closed subset of T is constructible, and noetherian itself.
(2) If T admits a ï¬nite stratiï¬cation with noetherian strata, then T is noetherian.
(3) Assume that X is a topologically noetherian scheme, and Y â†’X Â´etale. Then Y is topologically
noetherian.
Proof.
(1) Quasicompacity of T is clear. Also, the property of being noetherian passes to closed subsets,
as well as to open subsets. Thus, all open subsets are quasicompact; this implies that all locally
closed subsets are constructible, and that T is quasiseparated. Every connected component is an
53

intersection of open and closed subsets; this intersection has to be eventually constant, so that every
connected component is open and closed. By quasicompacity, there are only ï¬nitely many.
(2) Under this assumption, any descending sequence of closed subsets becomes eventually constant on
any stratum, and thus constant itself.
(3) There is a stratiï¬cation of X over which Y â†’X is ï¬nite Â´etale. By (2), we may assume that Y â†’X
is ï¬nite Â´etale. Any closed Z âŠ‚Y gives rise to a function fZ : X â†’N, mapping any x âˆˆX to
the cardinality of the ï¬bre of Z above a geometric point above x. As Z â†’X is ï¬nite, the function
fZ is upper semicontinuous, i.e. for all n, {x | fZ(x) â‰¥n} âŠ‚X is closed. Moreover, all fZ are
bounded independently of Z (by the degree of Y â†’X). Given a descending sequence of Zâ€™s, one
gets a descending sequence of fZâ€™s. Thus, for any n, {x | fZ(x) â‰¥n} forms a descending sequence
of closed subsets of X, which becomes eventually constant. As there are only ï¬nitely many n of
interest, all these subsets are eventually constant. This implies that fZ is eventually constant, which
shows that Z is eventually constant, as desired.
â–¡
Here is the promised result.
Proposition 6.6.11. Let X be a topologically noetherian scheme. A complex K âˆˆD(XproÂ´et, bR) is con-
structible if and only if there exists a ï¬nite stratiï¬cation {Xi Ö’â†’X} with K|Xi locally constant with perfect

7. THE PRO-Â´ETALE FUNDAMENTAL GROUP
We study the fundamental group resulting from the category of locally constant sheaves on the pro-Â´etale
topology, and explain how it overcomes some shortcomings of the classical Â´etale fundamental group for
non-normal schemes. The relevant category of sheaves, together with some other geometric incarnations,
is studied in Â§7.3, while the fundamental group is constructed in Â§7.4. However, we ï¬rst isolate a class of
topological groups Â§7.1; this class is large enough to contain the fundamental group we construct, yet tame
enough to be amenable to a formalism of â€œinï¬niteâ€ Galois theory introduced in Â§7.2.
7.1. Noohi groups. All topological groups in this section are assumed Hausdorff, unless otherwise speci-
ï¬ed. We study the following class of groups, with a view towards constructing the pro-Â´etale fundamental
group:
Deï¬nition 7.1.1. Fix a topological group G. Let G-Set be the category of discrete sets with a continuous
G-action, and let FG : G-Set â†’Set be the forgetful functor. We say that G is a Noohi group7 if the natural
map induces an isomorphism G â‰ƒAut(FG) of topological groups, where Aut(FG) is topologized using
the compact-open topology on Aut(S) for each S âˆˆSet.
The basic examples of Noohi groups are:
Example 7.1.2. If S is a set, then G := Aut(S) is a Noohi group under the compact-open topology; recall
that a basis of open neighbourhoods of 1 âˆˆAut(S) in the compact-open topology is given by the stabilizers
UF âŠ‚G of ï¬nite subsets F âŠ‚S. The natural map G â†’Aut(FG) is trivially injective. For surjectivity, any
Ï† âˆˆAut(FG) induces a Ï†S âˆˆG as S is naturally a G-set. It is therefore enough to show that any transitive
G-set is a G-equivariant subset of Sn for some n. Any transitive G-set is of the form G/UF for some ï¬nite
subset F âŠ‚S ï¬nite. For such F, the G-action on the given embedding F Ö’â†’S deï¬nes a G-equivariant
inclusion G/UF â†’Map(F, S), so the claim follows.
It is often non-trivial to check that a topological group with some â€œintrinsicâ€ property, such as the property
of being proï¬nite or locally compact, is a Noohi group. To systematically deal with such issues, we relate
Noohi groups to more classical objects in topological group theory: complete groups.
Deï¬nition 7.1.3. For a topological group G, we deï¬ne the completion Gâˆ—of G as the completion of G for
its two-sided uniformity, and write i : G Ö’â†’Gâˆ—for the natural embedding. We say G is complete if i is an
isomorphism.
We refer the reader to [AT08] for more on topological groups, especially [AT08, Â§3.6] for the existence
and uniqueness of completions. We will show that a topological group is Noohi if and only if it admits
enough open subgroups and is complete. In preparation, we have:

7.2. Inï¬nite Galois theory. Inï¬nite Galois theory gives conditions on a pair (C, F : C â†’Set), consisting
of a category C and a functor F, to be equivalent to a pair (G-Set, FG : G-Set â†’Set) for G a topological
group. Here, an object X âˆˆC is called connected if it is not empty (i.e., initial), and for every subobject
Y âŠ‚X (i.e., Y
âˆ¼
â†’Y Ã—X Y ), either Y is empty or Y = X.
Deï¬nition 7.2.1. An inï¬nite Galois category8 is a pair (C, F : C â†’Set) satisfying:
(1) C is a category admitting colimits and ï¬nite limits.
(2) Each X âˆˆC is a disjoint union of connected objects.
(3) C is generated under colimits by a set of connected objects.
(4) F is faithful, conservative, and commutes with colimits and ï¬nite limits.
The fundamental group of (C, F) is the topological group Ï€1(C, F) := Aut(F), topologized by the compact-
open topology on Aut(S) for any S âˆˆSet.
Example 7.2.2. If G is a Noohi group, then (G-Set, FG) is a Noohi category, and Ï€1(C, F) = G.
However, not all inï¬nite Galois categories arise in this way:
Example 7.2.3. There are coï¬ltered inverse systems Gi, i âˆˆI, of free abelian groups with surjective
transition maps such that the inverse limit G = lim Gi has only one element, cf. [HS54]. One can deï¬ne
an inï¬nite Galois category (C, F) as the 2-categorical direct limit of Gi-Set. It is not hard to see that
Ï€1(C, F) = lim Gi, which has only one element, yet F : C â†’Set is not an equivalence.
This suggests the following deï¬nition.
Deï¬nition 7.2.4. An inï¬nite Galois category (C, F) is tame if for any connected X âˆˆC, Ï€1(C, F) acts
transitively on F(X).
The main result is:
Theorem 7.2.5. Fix an inï¬nite Galois category (C, F) and a Noohi group G. Then

group G. There are even more serious issues, see Example 7.2.3.

(1) Ï€1(C, F) is a Noohi group.
(2) There is a natural identiï¬cation of Homcont(G, Ï€1(C, F)) with the groupoid of functors C â†’G-Set
that commute with the ï¬bre functors.
(3) If (C, F) is tame, then F induces an equivalence C â‰ƒÏ€1(C, F)-Set.
Proof. Fix a set Xi âˆˆC, i âˆˆI, of connected generators. As in the proof of Proposition 7.1.5, one gets that
Ï€1(C, F) is the closed subgroup of Q
i Aut(F(Xi)) of those elements compatible with all maps between all
Xi. It follows that Ï€1(C, F) is closed in a Noohi group, and thus a Noohi group itself, proving (1). Also,
part (2) is completely formal.
It remains to prove part (3). As (C, F) is tame, we know that for any connected X âˆˆC, Ï€1(C, F) acts
transitively on F(X). It follows that the functor C â†’Ï€1(C, F)-Set preserves connected components. By
interpreting maps f : Y â†’X in terms of their graph Î“f âŠ‚Y Ã— X, one sees that the functor is fully
faithful. For essential surjectivity, take any open subgroup U âŠ‚Ï€1(C, F)-Set. As Ï€1(C, F) is closed in
Q
i Aut(F(Xi)), there are ï¬nitely many Xij, with points xj âˆˆF(Xij), j âˆˆJ, such that U contains the
subgroup U â€² of Ï€1(C, F) ï¬xing all xj. The element Ï€1(C, F)/U â€² âˆˆÏ€1(C, F)-Set is the image of some
XUâ€² âˆˆC, as it can be realized as the connected component of Q

â–¡
Proposition 7.1.5 is useful to study Noohi groups under limits. Similarly, Theorem 7.2.5 is useful for
studying the behaviour under colimits. For example, one has coproducts:
Example 7.2.6. The category of Noohi groups admits coproducts. Indeed, if G and H are Noohi groups,
then we can deï¬ne an inï¬nite Galois category (C, F) as follows: C is the category of triples (S, ÏG, ÏH)
where S âˆˆSet, while ÏG : G â†’Aut(S) and ÏH : H â†’Aut(S) are continuous actions on S of G and H
respectively, and F : C â†’Set is given by (S, ÏG, ÏH) 7â†’S. One has an obvious map from the coproduct of
abstract groups G âˆ—H to Ï€1(C, F), from which one can see that (C, F) is tame. Then G âˆ—N H := Ï€1(C, F)
is a coproduct of G and H in the category of Noohi groups.
Remark 7.2.7. It may be true that general inï¬nite Galois categories are classiï¬ed by certain group objects
G in the pro-category of sets. One has to assume that the underlying pro-set of this group can be chosen to
be strict, i.e. with surjective transition maps. In that case, one can deï¬ne G-Set as the category of sets S
equipped with an action of G (i.e., equipped with a map GÃ— S â†’S in the pro-category of sets that satisï¬es
the usual axioms). It is easy to verify that G-Set forms an inï¬nite Galois category under the strictness
hypothesis. To achieve uniqueness of G, one will again have to impose the condition that there are enough
open subgroups. Fortunately, the inï¬nite Galois categories coming from schemes will be tame, so we do not
worry about such esoteric objects!
7.3. Locally constant sheaves. Fix a scheme X which is locally topologically noetherian. We will consider
the following classes of sheaves on XproÂ´et:
Deï¬nition 7.3.1. Fix F âˆˆShv(XproÂ´et). We say that F is
(1) locally constant if there exists a cover {Xi â†’X} in XproÂ´et with F|Xi constant.
(2) locally weakly constant if there exists a cover {Yi â†’X} in XproÂ´et with Yi qcqs such that F|Yi is
the pullback of a classical sheaf on the proï¬nite set Ï€0(Yi).
(3) a geometric covering if F is an Â´etale X-scheme satisfying the valuative criterion of properness.
We write LocX, wLocX and CovX for the corresponding full subcategories of Shv(XproÂ´et).
Remark 7.3.2. The objects of LocX, wLocX and CovX are classical. This is evident for CovX, and follows
from Lemma 5.1.4 for LocX and wLocX.
Remark 7.3.3. Any Y âˆˆCovX is quasiseparated: Y is locally topologically noetherian by Lemma 6.6.10.
Hence, we can write Y as a ï¬ltered colimit of its qcqs open subschemes. This remark will be used without
comment in the sequel.
Remark 7.3.4. Fix an F âˆˆShv(XproÂ´et). One checks that F âˆˆwLocX if and only if for any qcqs w-
contractible Y âˆˆXproÂ´et, the restriction F|Y is classical, and the pullback of its pushforward to Ï€0(Y ). For

such Y , pushforward and pullback along Shv(YÂ´et) â†’Shv(Ï€0(Y )Â´et), as well as the inclusion Shv(YÂ´et) âŠ‚

Example 7.3.5. If X = Spec(k) is the spectrum of a ï¬eld, then LocX = wLocX = CovX = Shv(XÂ´et).

Example 7.3.12. Some topological condition on the scheme X (besides being connected) is necessary
to make coverings well-behaved. Indeed, consider the following example. Let T be topological space
underlying the adic space corresponding to the closed unit disc over Qp. This is a spectral space, so there
is some ring A for which X = SpecA is homeomorphic to T. All arguments in the following are purely
topological, so we will argue on the side of T. The origin 0 âˆˆT is a closed point which admits no
generalizations, yet T is connected. One has open subsets T1, T1/2, . . . âŠ‚T, where T1/i is the annulus with
outer radius 1/i and inner radius 1/(i + 1).
The open subset U = T \ {0} âŠ‚T deï¬nes an object of CovX. Indeed, it is clearly Â´etale, and it satisï¬es
the valuative criterion of properness, as 0 does not admit nontrivial generalizations. One can show that U
also deï¬nes an object of wLocX, however it is not hard to see that U does not deï¬ne an object of LocX. We
claim that the disjoint union of U with an inï¬nite disjoint union of copies of X belongs to LocX. This will
prove that LocX is not closed under taking connected components, so that it cannot deï¬ne an inï¬nite Galois
category.
Consider the pro-Â´etale cover Y â†’X which has connected components Ï€0(Y ) = {0, 1, 1/2, 1/3, . . .},
with connected components Y0 = {0}, Y1/i = U1/i; it is easy to see how to build Y as an inverse limit. The
pullback of U to Y is the pullback of the sheaf FU on Ï€0(Y ) concentrated on {1, 1/2, 1/3, . . .}. To show
that the disjoint union of U with an inï¬nite disjoint union of copies of X belongs to LocX, it is enough
to show that the disjoint union of FU with an inï¬nite constant sheaf on Ï€0(Y ) is again an inï¬nite constant
sheaf. This boils down to some easy combinatorics on the proï¬nite set Ï€0(Y ), which we leave to the reader.

7.4. Fundamental groups. In this section, we assume that X is locally topologically noetherian and con-
nected, and we ï¬x a geometric point x of X with evx : LocX â†’Set being the associated functor F 7â†’Fx.
Lemma 7.4.1. The pair (LocX, evx) is an inï¬nite Galois category. Moreover, it is tame.
Proof. For the ï¬rst axiom, Remark 7.3.4 shows that wLocX âŠ‚Shv(XproÂ´et) is closed under colimits and ï¬-
nite limits. For the second axiom, we use CovX. Indeed, any Y âˆˆCovX is locally topologically noetherian,
so that its connected components are clopen. Any clopen subset of Y deï¬nes another object of CovX. It is a
connected object. Indeed, assume Y âˆˆCovX is connected as a scheme, and Z â†’Y is some map in CovX.
The image of Z is open and closed under specializations (by the valuative criterion of properness). As Y
is locally topologically noetherian, open implies locally constructible, and in general, locally constructible
and closed under specializations implies closed. Thus, the image of Z is open and closed, and thus either
empty or all of Y . The third axiom regarding things being a set (as opposed to a proper class) is left to the
reader. For the last axiom, we use LocX. As any pair of points of X is linked by a chain of specializations,
one checks that evx is conservative and faithful on LocX. As evx is given by evaluation on a connected w-
contractible object, it commutes with all colimits and all limits in Shv(XproÂ´et), and hence with all colimits
and ï¬nite limits in LocX.
Finally, we have to prove tameness. This comes down to showing that Ï€1 is large enough, i.e. we have to
construct enough paths in X. Thus, choose some connected cover Y â†’X, and any two geometric points
y1, y2 above x. They give rise to topological points Â¯y1, Â¯y2 âˆˆY . As Y is locally topologically noetherian, we
can ï¬nd a paths Â¯y1 = Â¯z0, Â¯z1, . . . , Â¯zn = Â¯y2 of points in Y such that for each i = 0, . . . , n âˆ’1, Â¯zi+1 is either
a specialization or a generalization of Â¯zi. Fix geometric points zi above Â¯zi. By projection, we get geometric
points xi of X, lying above topological points Â¯xi âˆˆX.
For each i, choose a valuation ring Ri with algebraically closed fraction ï¬eld, together with a map
SpecRi â†’Y such that the special and generic point are (isomorphic to) zi and zi+1 (or the other way
around); we ï¬x the isomorphisms. The valuation rings Ri induce maps SpecRi â†’X, which induce iso-
morphisms of ï¬bre functors evxi â‰ƒevxi+1. By composition, we get an isomorphism of ï¬bre functors
evx = evx0 â‰ƒevx1 â‰ƒ. . . â‰ƒevxn = evx ,
i.e. an automorphism Î³ âˆˆÏ€1(LocX, evx) of the ï¬bre functor evx. By construction, we have Î³(y1) = y2,
